\begin{itemize}

\[

<score>S</score>

Let's try to analyze the game systematically. The game is a two-player game with perfect information, and the moves are bounded by linear and quadratic constraints. It resembles a "game of resource allocation" where each player wants to keep the sum or sum of squares low, but also try to push the opponent into a corner. Usually such games have a threshold where the first player can force a win if the bound is large enough.

We need to consider optimal play. This is a game where each player chooses numbers that are "safe" for the opponent's next move. This is reminiscent of a "pursuit-evasion" or "resource allocation" game. Possibly we can formulate it as: At each turn, the player can choose a number within an interval. The game continues indefinitely if there is a strategy for each player to always choose a number that keeps the game within bounds. But since one player is trying to force a win, we need to see if one can force the opponent to violate the bound.

For Alice at odd n, she needs to choose x_n such that x_n ≤ A_n. For Bazza at even n, he needs to choose x_n such that x_n ≤ sqrt(B_n). So each player's move increases S and Q, thereby reducing the slack for themselves and the opponent? Actually after Alice's move, S_n increases, so A_n becomes λ n - S_n. But note that for the next turn, the slack for Bazza is B_{2k} = 2k - Q_{2k}. Since Q_{2k-1} is known, and Bazza chooses x_{2k} which increases Q, so his slack decreases.

Another idea: This game resembles the "game of two piles" where each player can remove or add chips. Here the constraints are on sums. Possibly we can transform it to a "pursuit" by using the fact that the sum of squares constraint is like a "norm" constraint. If we think of the sequence of numbers as a vector in R^n, the conditions are that the L1 norm is bounded by λ n and the L2 norm is bounded by sqrt(n). Since for any vector, the L2 norm is at most the L1 norm, but here we have the opposite: L1 ≤ λ n, L2 ≤ sqrt(n). So there is a relationship: λ n ≥ S_n, and n ≥ Q_n. So we need to see if one player can force the other to violate.

We can try to find a "strategy" for Bazza that ensures he can always move, i.e., that the game can continue indefinitely, for λ less than some threshold. But Bazza wants to force Alice to lose, so he wants to create a situation where S_{n-1} > λ n. So he needs to make the sum S large. He can do this by choosing large numbers on his turns, but that also increases Q, which may limit his future moves. So he needs to see if he can always keep Q within bound while making S large.

Let's attempt to find a "safe" strategy for Bazza that guarantees that he never loses, i.e., that he can always choose a number that satisfies Q_n ≤ n, and also that he can eventually force Alice to be unable to move. This is like a "winning strategy" for Bazza. For him to win, he must ensure that at some odd turn, S_{n-1} > λ n. That means that before his move, the sum S might be large enough that Alice cannot move.

We can think of the game as: At each turn, the player chooses a number within an interval. The intervals are determined by the state. The game is deterministic; we can analyze the "worst-case" for the player who wants to survive.

Let’s denote the state after n turns: (S, Q). The parity determines whose turn it is. The player can choose any number in [0, L], where L = λ n - S if it's Alice's turn, or L = sqrt(n - Q) if it's Bazza's turn. After the player chooses x, the new state is (S+x, Q+x^2) and the turn number increases by 1. The game ends if L < 0 (no legal move). So it's a kind of "game of moving in a space with constraints".

We want to know for which λ Alice can force a win (i.e., she can make Bazza's turn impossible). This is reminiscent of a "resource allocation" game where the players control different resources.

One possible method: Consider the "best possible" for each player to keep the game going. Define the "safety" of the state for the player whose turn it is. For example, suppose it's Alice's turn at n=2k-1. She can choose x. If she chooses x = λ - S_{2k-2}? Actually she can choose up to λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}. But S_{2k-2} is after even turn. She wants to set x to something that will force Bazza to lose later. Possibly she will try to maximize Q_n - n? But she must also keep S_n within bound. Actually she can choose x to be the maximum allowed, which is λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}. That would set S_{2k-1}=λ(2k-1). Then she increases Q by x^2. Then Bazza at n=2k must choose x_{2k} such that Q_{2k} ≤ 2k. That means x_{2k} ≤ sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}). If Q_{2k-1} > 2k, then Bazza loses immediately. So if she can make Q_{2k-1} > 2k, she wins. So she wants to make Q_{2k-1} as large as possible. Given that she can choose x_{2k-1} up to λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}, she can set x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2} to maximize S and also the square? Actually for a fixed S_n, the square sum Q_n is maximized by making one number as large as possible and the rest zero. So if she wants to increase Q, she should put all the allowed sum into a single number, i.e., choose x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}, and set all previous numbers? But the previous numbers are already fixed; she cannot change them. So the square sum Q_{2k-1} = Q_{2k-2} + (λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2})^2. She can also choose to set x_{2k-1} to a smaller number, but then Q_{2k-1} would be smaller, which might be worse for her win. So she will always choose the maximum allowed x_n on her turns to maximize Q and also S? But note: if she chooses the maximum, S_n becomes exactly λ n, which may be larger than necessary. However, if she does that, then Bazza's next move might be forced to have S_{2k} large, which could be bad for her later. So she must consider the overall strategy. She may want to keep S low to avoid giving Bazza the opportunity to push her to lose later. But she also wants to increase Q. So there is a trade-off: to increase Q, she needs to choose a large x, which increases S, which helps Bazza later. So she might want to choose a moderate x to increase Q but not too much S.

Thus the game is about finding the optimal "balance" for each player.

Maybe we can solve it by analyzing the "potential" function: V_n = (S_n)^2 - n Q_n. As noted, V_n ≤ 0. This quantity is a measure of "how much" the numbers are spread out. If the numbers are all equal, V_n = 0. If one number is large and the rest small, V_n is negative. For a fixed n, the smallest possible V_n given S_n and Q_n constraints? Actually we have S_n and Q_n; V_n is determined. But we can consider the change in V when a player moves.

Suppose it's Alice's turn at n (odd). She chooses x ≥ 0 such that S_{n-1} + x ≤ λ n. After her move, V_n = (S_{n-1}+x)^2 - n Q_{n-1} - n x^2? Wait careful: Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x^2. So V_n = (S_{n-1}+x)^2 - n (Q_{n-1}+x^2). V_{n-1} = (S_{n-1})^2 - (n-1) Q_{n-1}. We can compute the difference: V_n - V_{n-1} = (S_{n-1}+x)^2 - n Q_{n-1} - n x^2 - (S_{n-1}^2 - (n-1) Q_{n-1}) = S_{n-1}^2 + 2 S_{n-1}x + x^2 - n Q_{n-1} - n x^2 - S_{n-1}^2 + (n-1) Q_{n-1} = 2 S_{n-1} x + x^2 - n Q_{n-1} - n x^2 + (n-1) Q_{n-1} = 2 S_{n-1} x + x^2 - n x^2 - Q_{n-1} = 2 S_{n-1} x + (1-n)x^2 - Q_{n-1}. So V_n = V_{n-1} + 2 S_{n-1} x + (1-n)x^2 - Q_{n-1}. Since V_{n-1} ≤ 0, this may be negative or positive. Not obviously monotonic.

Perhaps we can think about the "safe" region for the state (S, Q) given parity. Define the "danger" as the smallest n such that the player cannot move. For Alice, she loses if S_{n-1} > λ n at her turn. Actually she needs S_{2k} ≤ λ (2k+1) for her to be able to choose x_{2k+1} ≥ 0 such that S_{2k+1} ≤ λ (2k+1). If S_{2k} > λ (2k+1), then she cannot move, so Bazza would have forced a win if he made S_{2k} that large. So Bazza's goal is to make S_{2k} > λ (2k+1) at some k. Similarly, Alice's goal is to make Q_{2k-1} > 2k at some k.

Thus the game can be seen as: At each step, the player chooses a number, and we check if the "margin" is enough. It's like a "pursuit" where the two players are "pushing" the constraints.

Maybe we can find a "strategy" for Bazza that ensures that for all k, S_{2k} ≤ λ (2k+1) and also that he can eventually make S_{2k} > λ (2k+1) if λ is small enough. Similarly, for Alice.

Let's attempt to analyze the "worst-case" for Alice when Bazza tries to make S large. Suppose Bazza chooses the maximum possible x_{2k} at each even turn. Then S_{2k} = S_{2k-1} + sqrt(2k - Q_{2k-1}). Given that Alice controls her previous choices, we need to see if she can keep S_{2k-1} low enough that even with Bazza's maximal move, S_{2k} ≤ λ (2k+1). If she can, then she survives. She might try to "bait" Bazza by making her numbers large to increase Q, but then S also increases, which helps Bazza. So there is a balance.

Maybe we can think of the "optimal" strategy for each player as: they will try to keep the "slack" for themselves as large as possible, and they will also try to reduce the slack of the opponent. This is similar to a "Nash equilibrium" in a game with linear constraints.

Actually the pattern we observed: the increment per Alice turn is always 1.4? Let's see: We started with x1=0.7, then after a series of turns, S after odd n is λ n = 1.4 * n? Actually we set x_n = λ? But in this simulation, we are not using a fixed x_n; we are choosing x_n to be the maximum allowed? Wait we need to re-examine the strategy for λ=0.7. We set x1=0.7 (the maximum allowed). Then Bazza at n=2 chooses x2 = sqrt(2 - 0.49)= sqrt(1.51)=1.2288 to maximize S2. Then Alice at n=3: she has S2=1.9288, λ*3=2.1, so she can choose x3 up to 2.1-1.9288=0.1712. Actually she chooses x3=0.1712, which is the maximum possible given her sum constraint. But note that the sum constraint is S3 ≤ 3λ=2.1, and S2=1.9288, so the maximum she can choose is 0.1712. We can try to find a recurrence. Let’s denote after an even turn 2k, the state is (S_{2k}, Q_{2k}) with Q_{2k}=2k (if Bazza plays optimally, he can always set Q_{2k}=2k). Then Alice at turn 2k+1 chooses x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} (max allowed). So S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1). Then Q_{2k+1}=Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2 = 2k + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2. Then Bazza at turn 2k+2 chooses x_{2k+2}=sqrt(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}). So S_{2k+2}=S_{2k+1}+sqrt(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}). And Q_{2k+2}=2k+2. So the process is determined by the initial values.

We need to compute S_{2k} for k≥1. The initial step: at n=0, S=0, Q=0. Alice at n=1 chooses x1 = λ (max allowed). So S1=λ, Q1=λ^2. Then Bazza at n=2 chooses x2 = sqrt(2 - Q1) = sqrt(2 - λ^2). So S2 = λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2), Q2=2. Then Alice at n=3 chooses x3 = λ*3 - S2 = 3λ - (λ + sqrt(2-λ^2)) = 2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2). So S3=3λ, Q3=2 + (2λ - sqrt(2-λ^2))^2. Then Bazza at n=4 chooses x4 = sqrt(4 - Q3). So S4 = 3λ + sqrt(4 - Q3), Q4=4. And so on.

So we can compute S_{2k} as a function of λ. The game ends when at some odd turn 2k+1, the allowed x_{2k+1} is negative, i.e., λ(2k+1) < S_{2k}. That is when S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). So we need to see for which λ does this happen at some k. If it never happens, then the game can continue indefinitely, and neither wins? But note that if it never happens, then Alice always has a legal move, and Bazza always has a legal move because he always sets Q_{2k}=2k which is within the bound. So the game continues forever, and no winner. In that case, neither player has a winning strategy? But the problem asks for which λ Alice has a winning strategy and which λ Bazza has a winning strategy. Possibly for some λ, Alice can force a win; for others, Bazza can force a win; and for some, no one can force a win (draw). We need to determine all λ.

We have a two-player game with perfect information. The state is (S, Q, parity). The players choose numbers within intervals. The game ends when a player cannot move. We want to know for which λ does Alice have a winning strategy (i.e., she can force a win regardless of Bazza's moves). Similarly for Bazza.

Since the game is infinite-horizon, we can think of it as a "positional game" where the players try to create a "dangerous" state for the opponent. The outcome might be determined by the "capacity" of each player to control the constraints.

Given the structure, perhaps we can solve it by analyzing the "safety" of the state in terms of a potential function. Let’s define the "margin" for Alice: M_A = λ n - S_n. For Bazza: M_B = n - Q_n. At her turn, she can choose any x such that M_A ≥ x. At his turn, he can choose any x such that M_B ≥ x^2. The game is like a "resource" game where each player has a resource that is increased by their opponent's moves.

We can consider the "critical" value where the game becomes "draw" (infinite play) as the threshold where the maximum strategy leads to a periodic pattern that is safe. Then Alice's next move may be larger, causing Q to increase more, and eventually Bazza might be able to force a win by making the margin negative. So the optimal play for Bazza is to find a strategy that maximizes his chance of winning. He wants to make the margin for Alice become negative at some odd turn. So we need to analyze the game as a dynamic game.

Given the complexity, we might approach the problem by defining a "potential" function that is monotonic and bounded, and then determine the outcome based on the "critical" value.

Another idea: This game is equivalent to a "game of two piles" where each move adds a number to a pile, and the opponent's pile has a constraint. Possibly we can use the concept of "strategy stealing" or "symmetry". But we need to classify all λ.

Let's try to systematically analyze the game. The game is determined by the state (S, Q, parity). The players choose numbers within intervals. The game ends when a player cannot move. This is a kind of "resource allocation" game where each player controls a "budget" of linear and quadratic sums.

We can try to think of it as a "pursuit" where each player tries to make the opponent's slack negative. The slack for Alice is A = λ n - S_n. For Bazza, it's B = n - Q_n. At her turn, she can choose any x such that x ≤ A. After her move, the new slack for Bazza becomes B' = (n+1) - (Q_n + x^2) = (n+1 - Q_n) - x^2 = (n+1 - Q_n) - x^2. But note that n+1 is the new even number? Actually after her move, it's an odd turn, so the number of turns is odd. The next turn is Bazza's, and he will have to satisfy that his chosen x^2 ≤ (n+1) - (Q_n + x^2). So his slack after her move is (n+1) - (Q_n + x^2). So we have a game where at each step, the player chooses a number within a certain range, and the opponent's range is determined by the new total.

We can think of the game as a "tug-of-war" where each player controls a "resource" that is increased by the opponent's moves. Perhaps we can transform it to a "discrete time" game with a "potential" function: V = (λ n - S_n) + (n - Q_n) or something like that.

Given the recurrence we derived for the maximal strategies, we can analyze the stability. For λ > 1/sqrt(2), the maximal strategy leads to a draw (infinite). But we need to check if either player can force a win by deviating. For λ > 1/sqrt(2), we saw that Bazza cannot force a win because any move he makes leads to a positive margin for Alice, and the recurrence seems to keep it positive. But is it possible for Bazza to force a win by making a move that gives Alice a small margin and then later, by a different move, he can make the margin negative? For instance, suppose he chooses x2 such that d1 is very small, then d2 = 2λ - sqrt(2-d1^2). If d1 is small, sqrt(2-d1^2) ≈ sqrt(2), so d2 ≈ 2λ - sqrt(2). For λ=0.8, 2λ=1.6, sqrt(2)=1.414, d2≈0.186. Then d3 = 2λ - sqrt(2-d2^2) ≈ 1.6 - sqrt(2-0.0346)=1.6-1.399=0.201, etc. So it converges to 0.2. So no negative. If he chooses x2 such that d1 is large? d1 = 2λ - a, with a ≤ sqrt(2-λ^2). For λ=0.8, sqrt(2-λ^2)=1.166, so max d1 = 2λ - 0 = 1.6. But a=0 gives d1=1.6, then sqrt(2-d1^2)= sqrt(2-2.56) imaginary, so that's not possible because then Q3 = Q2 + d1^2 = 0.64 + 2.56 = 3.2 >4? Let's analyze: For given λ, Alice chooses x1. Bazza chooses a. Then d1 = 3λ - (λ+a) = 2λ - a. Then after his move at turn 4, we have d2 = 5λ - S4, where S4 = S3 + x4, and x4 = sqrt(4 - Q3). Q3 = a^2 + d1^2. So d2 = 5λ - (3λ + sqrt(4 - (a^2+d1^2))) = 2λ - sqrt(4 - (a^2+d1^2)). Since d1 = 2λ - a, we have a^2 + d1^2 = a^2 + (2λ - a)^2 = 2a^2 -4λa +4λ^2. So d2 = 2λ - sqrt(4 - 2a^2 +4λa -4λ^2). So d2 becomes negative if sqrt(4 - 2a^2 +4λa -4λ^2) > 2λ, i.e., 4 - 2a^2 +4λa -4λ^2 > 4λ^2 => 4 - 2a^2 +4λa -8λ^2 > 0 => 2a^2 -4λa +8λ^2 -4 < 0 => a^2 -2λa +4λ^2 -2 < 0.
